B0059XTV4Y|1.0|Wallis Warfield Simpson was one of the most controversial figures of the 20th century -- a twice-divorced American whose love affair with King Edward VIII led to his abdication.<br /><br />So you would THINK that even Madonna could wring a decent dramatic movie out of the woman. Some passion, some intensity, some controversy... SOMETHING. But \\"W.E.\\" ends up being a choppy, jumbled affair that leaves you desperately hoping for something other than total fluff. And you never, ever get it.<br /><br />It's not actually the story of Wallis herself, but of Wally Winthrop (Abbie Cornish), the wife of a wealthy doctor in present-day New York. Wally's husband neglects her (he's obviously having an affair) and doesn't want to have a baby with her, so she wanders to Sotheby's to ogle the for-sale possessions of the legendary Duchess of Windsor.<br /><br />She also falls in love with an attentive Russian guard after a savage beatdown by her husband. For some reason, she also starts... hallucinating? Seeing visions? Communing with the ghost of Wallis?<br /><br />Well, whatever happened, she starts seeing Wallis' (Andrea Riseborough) past -- her introduction to the Prince of Wales (James D'Arcy), the affair that sprang up, the glamorous life they had together, and his obsessive love for her that led him to abdicate, even though Wallis was desperate for him not to do so.<br /><br />Anyone expecting a semi-balanced depiction of Wallis and Edward should keep looking, because  \\"W.E.\\" is basically a cinematic fanfic written by a starry-eyed Wallis fangirl. Wallis and Edward are shiny, perfect, idealized dolls who are everything noble and glamorous, and anyone who dislikes them is just a mean ol' hater who irrationally loathes poor wittle Wallis.<br /><br />In fact, Madonna is so wedded to this depiction that she either ignores (Wallis' other affairs, her botched abortion) or dismisses (Nazi sympathies) every imperfection that either of them had. She tries to justify it with a cache of fictional \\"secret\\" letters, but it all rings hollow.<br /><br />Sadly, the rest of the movie is all flashy, glamorous prettiness with no substance. Madonna's direction is a constant assault of choppy, fragmented edits -- for instance, an intimate kiss between Wallis and Edward is interrupted by a random pan right up a palm tree. The most surreal? A drug-fueled scene where Wallis hikes up her dress and does a suggestive dance with another woman... IN FRONT OF A CROWD.<br /><br />And on the rare occasions when Madonna DOES try to give us a deep emotional moment, it comes across as artificial and clumsy (\\"People seem to only focus on what he gave up for her. But what about what she gave up?\\"). It's like a teenage \\"Twilight\\" fan directed this.<br /><br />As the final indignity, Wallis' complex story is just window-dressing for the story of Wally. Madonna tries to ram the \\"similarities\\" down our throat (Wally's rotten husband is referred to as \\"our own Prince of Wales\\"), but in the end there is almost nothing in common between these two women. So, they're alike because their marriages didn't work? That's all? Who CARES about Wally?<br /><br />The actors... well, they do the best they can. Cornish is stiff and awkward, but Riseborough and D'Arcy do the best they can with their flimsily perfect characters. Unfortunately, the awful direction makes their dramatic scenes look ridiculous -- when Wallis runs into her bedroom to cry, Madonna manages to make the act of RUNNING look artificial.<br /><br />\\"W.E.\\" was booed at Cannes, and rightfully so -- it's a clumsy, hackneyed story that shows that Madonna is as bad a director as she is an actress. Stick with \\"Edward & Mrs. Simpson\\" for a more skilled, nuanced depiction.fictional \\"secret\\" letters, but it all rings hollow.<br /><br />Sadly, the rest of the movie is all flashy, glamorous prettiness with no substance. 0143121316|4.0|Henry Kissinger has incredibly unique insight into China's 20th Century history, and the chapters that touch on his personal experience are outstanding. As with any memoir, those sections should be taken with a grain of salt--but that hardly diminishes their impact. These chapters alone make the book a must read.<br /><br />The earlier sections on Tang dynasty history, The Art of War and Weiqi, are all similarly interesting, but somewhat more perplexing. At some point did Henry Kissinger really become an expert on 8th Century Chinese history? Probably not, but he nonetheless has drawn on a group of researchers that clearly do have excellent knowledge of these periods. If the book is somewhat long and overly lionizes the brilliance of the Chinese political mind (It's a little much to hear how Mao repeatedly bested the Soviets, Americans, Koreans, Vietnamese etc, while somehow not managing to secure any major concessions, like Taiwan, for example), it still provides excellent insight and covers a huge swath of Chinese history with excellent detail.<br /><br />Regardless of Kissinger's odd crush on China and apparent help writing the book, it's nonetheless worth a read.

1451648537|5.0|There's two main points I want to make about Walter Isaacson's biography of Steve Jobs. First, it's an outstanding book. It's undoubtedly the best biography I've ever read, and is among the best books I've read of any kind.<br /><br />Second, it's a LONG book. It's 571 pages, and it took me a good five weeks to read it (your mileage may vary). But it's long in a good way. It's filled with scores of interesting stories from Jobs' personal and professional lives, and packed with colorful detail. It's not a book to rush through; each section gives you something to stop and think about.<br /><br />Isaacson interviewed over 100 people for the book, allowing him to faithfully describe countless moments throughout Jobs' life. We see the strange alchemy that allowed Jobs and Steve Wozniak - two young men of virtually opposite personalities - to create a computer company called Apple. It's amusing to read how Jobs avoided bathing for a while as a young man, believing his vegetarian diet prevented body odor (he was wrong.) Later on, we see behind-the-scenes stories as Jobs butts heads with Michael Eisner, Bill Gates and other business heavyweights. One of the most amazing revelations of the book is that Jobs, who was adopted, unknowingly met his biological father, who owned a Silicon Valley restaurant.<br /><br />The book works on various levels. On one hand, it's a history book, recounting key events at Apple, Next Computer and Pixar, and the development of such signature products as the iPod, iTunes, and iPhone. While I lived through these events, and often followed the news coverage, I discovered that I really didn't know the full stories until I read this book. I was surprised to discover how close Jobs and John Sculley were at first, before Sculley ousted Jobs from Apple in 1985. I was amused to learn that it was the success of Pixar's \\"Toy Story\\" that in many ways saved Jobs from oblivion and gave him newfound influence.<br /><br />Also, the book is a fascinating look at business and management. Jobs brought a unique style to the workplace  - he was controlling, abrasive, demanding, and perfectionist.  Unlike companies such as Google and Microsoft, he did not like trying a lot of things to see what worked. Rather, he preferred to focus intently on making small numbers of products great. He was both a long-term visionary, and incredible micromanager, fussing over the small details of products and driving many of the people who worked with him crazy in the process. \\"His imaginative leaps were instinctive, unexpected, and at times magical,\\" writes Isaacson<br /><br />Third, the book is an intriguing look at a unique human being. He was abusive and bullying to many people, while still inspiring people to do great work. \\"He could size people up, understand their inner thoughts, and know how to relate to them, cajole them, or hurt them at will,\\" says Isaacson. For all his faults in interpersonal relations, I found myself admiring his passion for accomplishing great things. It's disconcerting to be reading about Jobs' brilliance with making products that are easy to use, while in daily life we are all frustrated by all sorts of poorly designed technology.<br /><br />As much as a I like this biography, no book is perfect and I have three small criticisms. First, the section in which Isaacson discusses Jobs' favorite music is completely unnecessary. Second, I wished at times that Isaacson included more dates in the book. Sometimes, I was trying to determine exactly when something happened, but it wasn't always clear.  Finally, I wish there were more pictures of the other people - besides Jobs and his family - who are mentioned repeatedly in the book.

For these authors, truly engaging their fans, energizing their readers and delivering with the same quality of their previous works becomes just too much trouble.  I had never subscribed to that skeptical view until I read this work, a piece that unfortunately diminishes the value of every great story Grisham has told before.  Save your money and your time -- read something else.

It is obvious from the get-go that the two cousins are spoiled brats, so you have a fair idea of just why Nanny McPhee shows up.  The five youngsters (obviously) do not get along at all, and in fact Nanny McPhee has to quite literally show them who's boss, which she does rather well.<br />There is more of her magic, but unlike Mary Poppins there are no songs to carry the story along and, in truth, they are not needed.<br />To add to the mother's problems, her smarmy brother-in-law is trying to get her to sell the farm.  There is an ulterior motive for his actions: he's in debt over his ears to a casino.  Worse, the casino owner has sent his &#34;Hit-men,&#34; actually two rather bloodthirsty women, to collect.<br />An interesting tidbit is the jackdaw that hangs around Nanny McPhee and hold conversations with her.  The bird is forever belching because it has a habit of eating window putty.  This bird, and several other things, move the show right along.  I must add that Dame Maggie Smith (remember her as Prof. McGonnagal in &#34;Harry Potter&#34;?) is absolutely delightful as the little old lady who runs the local store.  She (and several others) remember Nanny McPhee very kindly.  At the end of the film you find out why, but only if you saw the first film.<br />All in all, this is a truly delightful film, in spite of a little over-the-top acting on the part of the brother-in-law and the two ladies(?).  If you liked the first Nanny McPhee, you are going to love this one, too.  I do highly recommend it, and I'm very glad I have it.ting window putty. I have a X100, though my primary camera is a Mamiya 7 medium format film camera.<br /><br />Pros:<br />-awesome build quality, seems to match the X-Pro1 and certainly does fine on my X100<br />-takes AAAs...not quite as convenient as AAs but still a lot better than those crazy batteries that other similar flashes take.<br />-TTL metering on the Fuji cameras is quite good (though I use it mostly on manual)<br />-controlling the flash is a breeze. for this, I would give it 5 stars. really excellent, and easy to switch between manual and TTL.<br />-it works really conveniently as an off-camera flash, triggered by your camera's built-in flash. If the flash is already on and then you turn on the camera, the flash will go off. I'm not sure if my unit is defective, but that's pretty annoying. I discovered that if you turn them both on at the same time, they work.<br /><br />Overall, this flash functions pretty well on the Fuji cameras, once you get it turned on. I really wish this flash had an auto setting with a mini exposure chart on the back (like the sunpaks do! they can be used with any camera really easily). I guess if you only shoot digital, manual mode is a fine alternative (I use it plenty on film too). Is it the perfect flash for any camera? It's just okay for that. It's really small and portable, much stronger and higher up than the X100's flash, and would make a good addition to any kit. I use flash a lot, and this one is probably the best option for the X100 and X-Pro1 unless you need to be bouncing light.

B009L32R5I|3.0|Steve Hackett's second attempt at covering older Genesis tunes is more ambitious than the first edition but not quite as successful.  The first \\"Genesis Revisited\\" album was a more adventurous take on some classic Genesis songs with different singers bringing their own interpretations to these well-known songs.<br /><br />\\"Genesis Revisted II\\" loses the adventurous nature of the first one and replaced it with more straight-forward recreations of Genesis classics.  This sounds great in theory but Hackett attempts to recreate the songs almost exactly, to the point that there's little new to be entertained by.  This is further complicated by the fact that some of the singers have voices very similar to Peter Gabriel's or Phil Collins (including his son Simon who sings a verse during Supper's Ready).  I would have much preferred if some license was taken with the songs, either in the choice of singer or in the arrangements.<br /><br />Having said this, the songs are the star here and this album is like a greatest hits collection of Hackett-era Genesis.  Three stars are given for the material presented here but two stars taken away for the lost opportunity to do something really fun and adventurous.

1451638450|4.0|\\"Ivan, you idiot!\\" is the way that most of the other characters in the Vorkosigan series address Ivan Vorpatril, Miles Vorkosigan's cousin. Ivan isn't as sharp or as ambitious as his cousin Miles, but he isn't stupid. He's smart enough to realise that he's a potential claimant to the throne if anything happens to Emperor Gregor Vorbarra. On Barrayar, that makes him a magnet for plots: those who want to use him for their own ends, and those who want to prevent others from using him. He lacks enough wealth and power in his own right to protect himself, so he disguises himself as a non-entity: a Vor class playboy lacking ability and ambition.<br /><br />In previous Vorkosigan Saga novels, he's been absent or merely a bit player, a tall handsome Vor cousin to Miles, acting as comic relief and contrast to his brilliant cousin. He takes centre stage in this novel. Ivan's abhorred cousin Byerly Vorrutyer (previously appearing in A Civil Campaign IIRC) ropes Ivan into one of his plots. Shortly afterwards, Ivan is kidnapped by a pair of beautiful women and marries one of them, with the aid of a box of breakfast cereal, to prevent them both from getting arrested (trust me, it makes sense in the book). After that, things start to get complicated. The climax is a twist you will never see coming, but in retrospect makes perfect sense. It is at its heart a love story, and I found it a bit frustrating in parts, yelling \\"Just tell each other how you feel already!\\" at the screen (I read the Kindle edition). Not the strongest book in the series but still satisfying.

0525478817|5.0|This is a brilliant new young adult fiction novel, narrated by a sixteen-year-old cancer patient named Hazel Grace Lancaster.  This novel gives the reader a look through the eyes of someone who is surrounded by the symbolism and metaphoric resonance of death and the day to day, minute to minute struggles of living with lungs that fail to function on their own.  In the novel Hazel is forced to attend a cancer survivor support group where she subsequently meets a young man named Augustus Waters who, once upon a one time, had previously been diagnosed with osteosarcoma and had a leg amputated as a side effect of treatment.  The two of them embark down a path of to discover insights to life, death, love, and friendship.<br />John Green puts a great deal of effort into the story to make you laugh and cry and it's not uncommon in any of his books to be found doing both simultaneously.  Green has a way of giving you just enough of exactly what you want hear in the story to keep you satisfied without giving you what you want.  He keeps you on the edge of your seat at every moment with even the format the pages and chapter follow.  In Looking for Alaska, each chapter begins with a number of days that are subsequently counting down to some sort of event or story climax.  It continues on like this, 127 days before, 98 days before, 72 days before and so on before too long when you are so built up from wanting to know what was coming since the beginning.  You know something is going to happen but how come the characters in the novel don't?  It's frustrating and exciting at the same time but it locks you in and keeps you reading the story, page by page, day by day.<br />The Fault in Our Stars is a great addition to the long list of amazing young adult fictions read in the world.  Alongside Green's own novels (Will Grayson Will Grayson, Paper Towns, Looking for Alaska, and An Abundance of Katherines), The Fault in Our Stars has made a place for itself next to other big names in the young adult fiction genre including The Perks of Being a Wallflower by Stephen Chbosky, which is hands down one of the best young adult fictions of this time, and the more recent Hunger Games.  The Fault in Our Stars has even made a lasting impression strong enough to contend with other more influential novels not of our time such as Fahrenheit 451 by Ray Bradbury and The Great Gatsby by F. Scott Fitzgerald.<br />While I do compare Green's novel to others in a broad comparison or Green's fame alongside other names like Diana Wynne Jones and J.R.R. Tolkien, Green's writing is a contender of its own.  Green's characters are smart, pragmatic, quick witted, and have a great respect for knowledge.  Every novel he has written has some character that is beyond their years by how they are presented before any given situation whether it is solving the disappearance of a neighbor friend, understanding the reasons behind someone else's death, or reflecting on the unjustified correlation stereotyping eggs as being strictly a breakfast food.<br />Green's novel might be all these things and it may not be all these things to you as a reader, but one thing his novels will never not have is love.  He talks a great deal about love and its many splendors.  How it can surprise you and be somewhere you didn't expect, how love cannot be quantified or solved and that there is always some sort of leap involved.  My favorite quote from the Fault in Our Stars is in fact a quote about love narrated by Hazel Grace \\"I fell in love the way you fall asleep: slowly, then all at once.\\"<br />The Fault in Our Stars is a wonderful novel and it is my belief that everyone should read it despite preference, ability, and maybe even library availability.  Novels such as this, are of the kinds that shape generations and steamroll prejudice, make changes to our world and build monuments in our lives.  Novels like this bring people closer together and help them feel something that maybe wasn't there before,or was small enough to remain unfelt or unacknowledged despite the feelings presence.  When I read a good book I fall in love with it.  That book becomes the only thing I can talk about for days or weeks or even years depending on its value to me.  The Fault in Our Stars was one of those novels for me and I hope that anyone else who has or has yet to read it feels the same way.  It's just as John Green wrote in An Abundance of Katherines, \\"Books are the ultimate Dumpees: put them down and they'll wait for you forever; pay attention to them and they always love you back.\\"

B009FBX5M0|2.0|In the course of 007's 50 years in film, there have certainly been hits and misses in the scoring. When John Barry was at the helm, it was always a superb score. Barry defined the Bond sound. When others - like Marvin Hamlisch, Eric Serra, or Michael Kamen - had the reins, the results were poor. In 1997, Bond fans were treated to the work of David Arnold, who had scored such films as Stargate and Independence Day. Working in Barry's style and yet always ready to break fresh ground, between 1997 and 2008, Arnold's work always told you that you were listening to a James Bond score.<br /><br />A Bond score fan, I was worried when I heard Thomas Newman was taking over for this film. Newman is great, but how would he do at Bond music? When Adele's song was released, however, I was filled with a surge of wild optimism. The song was everything good about the Bond music. Sadly, when I received this score, my hopes were dashed.<br /><br />First, the Adele title song was not included - a major, major, major marketing mistake. But, with thirty tracks of music on the CD, we were sure to get some good James Bond music, right...? Um, no. The James Bond Theme is mostly buried... You should always know you are listening to a Bond score when you listen to it. In Casino Royale, the James Bond Theme was not let out of the box until the end, when your hair stands on end as Bond stands over White - but appropriately so, as it is the first \\"James Bond moment\\" with Daniel Craig. But the rest of the score had that \\"Bond sound\\" even without the primary theme. I mean, quite literally, Clint Mansell's score for Sahara (2005) sounds more like James Bond than this does!<br /><br />Worse, the Adele theme is almost nonexistent in the score. The album notes say it is in \\"Komodo Dragon\\", but I can hardly tell. This is a tremendous error, as weaving the title song(s) into the orchestral music has been a James Bond staple since 1963 and \\"From Russia With Love\\", and really ties the whole package together. Without strong themes, this score feels meandering and thoroughly aimless.<br /><br />The score also contains very little of the lush romanticism of John Barry or David Arnold (with the exception of a little in the Severine theme). While this score is decent as a standalone score for, say, a Bourne movie or some other film, it hardly sounds like a James Bond score and, as such, does not fit in the 007 catalogue... Certainly not on the genre's 50th anniversary.<br /><br />Very disappointing. Next time, David Arnold needs to make a triumphant return and fix the musical hash we have in this release. Or - and yes, I am being facitious - have Adele write the score, as she obviously knows what the Bond sound is more than Thomas Newman does.<br /><br />------- UPDATE -------<br />My initial review of the score still stands, but since writing it, I have seen the film. Despite the overwhelmingly positive critical reception and praise, and the incredible box office receipts the film is making, I was even more disappointed with the actual movie than the score. This was primarily because of its gaping plot holes and some startlingly out-of-character behavior by Bond.<br /><br />I do now see where the cues on the CD fit in to the overall framework, but I still don't like the way the score \\"works\\" in the film. Certainly much of this has to do with director Mendes' input and what he wanted, but the score just doesn't have enough of a James Bond feel - on the CD or in the film.<br /><br />To see if it's just me, I have now gone back and listened to a whole selection of Bond score CD's, including every one of the David Arnold entries. This score just doesn't hold a candle to the genre. I think some of it is the lack of an \\"echo\\" sound to the mix, as all the previous recordings had a rather \\"large-space\\" feel and this one was recorded in a different studio, but that is not all of it. Most of the action music just doesn't have enough of a strong, driving, brassy, big-orchestra sound (like \\"Exercise at Gibraltar\\" for The Living Daylights, \\"Miami Airport\\" for Casino Royale or \\"Time to Get Out\\" for Quantum of Solace, and so forth), and could have come from any modern spy flick.<br /><br />Meanwhile, there just isn't enough sweeping, romantic music (like \\"Bond Meets Tania\\" for From Russia With Love, \\"Mujahadin and Opium\\" for The Living Daylights, \\"Christmas in Turkey\\" for The World is Not Enough, \\"I'm the Money\\" or \\"City of Lovers\\" from Casino Royale, etc). I thought the trip up to Skyfall in the film, with all of that glorious scenery and some nice aerial footage, as well, would have made a terrific opportunity for some of the latter category; instead, it was too muted, too quiet, too non-Bond in feel.<br /><br />At any rate, I still feel that David Arnold needs to return with the classic Bond sound on the next film.

The point with Alexia was that she was not perfect and beautiful (at least as I remember it). I would have loved to see an artist draw a less-perfect character. Instead, Alexia is presented as a pretty lady with an overly generous bust and an hourglass figure. Hardly a unique or unflattering body type.<br /><br />As for the story, as others have mentioned a lot had to be cut from the written version (including some very useful explanations, such as what drones are), but I say enough of the story stays to make it enjoyable.<br /><br />All that being said, I will admit I read through it twice the first evening I had it (not only because I adore Lord Akeldama and Prof. Lyall) so I say it is an enjoyable and worthwhile purchase if you like graphic novels and you liked the book.

This album falls in between Get a Grip and Nine Lives in the balladometer, with 40% of the tracks being a ballad - to me, this kind of detracts from a hard rock/metal album.  None of the ballads have really caught my ear yet, but 4 of them are OK - the last track is probably the slowest thing the band has ever done - maybe it'll grow on me, but so far it's a dud; and the country singing of Carrie Underwood rather detracts from the song she sings on.  The rockers don't rock as much as those on 9 Lives, but they're pretty good.  Overall, it's a pretty enjoyable Aerosmith album, and worth buying if you like the band's other later works.  A consideration for most older bands (especially those we haven't heard from) - Steven Tyler's voice has held up pretty well over the years. Note the Walmart edition contains a good, energetic cover song that helps counter the overballadization, while the special edition tracks (I was able to listen to on youtube) are: a pretty good non-ballad with bassist Tom Hamilton on vocals, a slow song with Joe Perry on vocals which didn't impress, and yet another ballad with Steven Tyler on vocals.  If it was a jewel case I'd probably have gotten the special edition, but as I hate the digipaks, I didn't feel I missed much by not getting these songs.<br />  All in all, though, I'm pretty excited that even 60% of the album is worthy Aerosmith - they've still got it when they're not ballading. 0452273331|3.0|Review by Jack Trammell<br /><br />The Fountainhead is a brilliant novel, written by a brilliant thinker--but it has been greatly abused in the name of so-called Objectivism and in terms of Capitalism and Christianity.  While Howard Roark stands sublime and triumphant at the metaphorical pinnacle of modernism and individualism (at the top of his own architectural masterpiece, a skyscraper of steel rising above the ordinariness of the city below), his now obedient wife at his side (Dominique), and his enemies humbled (Gail Wynand) or banished (Ellsworth Toohey), the novel none-the-less ends with the central issues of modernity and of morality completely unresolved in terms of plot.<br /><br />This novel, taken as a whole and as philosophy, is in reality nothing less than a scathing indictment of Capitalism and of modernity, and antagonistic to the most basic tenants of Christianity.  Roark may indeed be a literary hero (and his counterpart, Peter Keeting, a literary goat), but in terms of realpolitik Roark represents selfishness, arrogance, and utter disdain for man as a social creature.  To quote one reviewer of the book, \\"it is better read when one is young enough to miss the point,\\" and the point according to the overarching storyline is that any individual man does not need any other man (or woman).  All of the characters, including Roark, engage in a Capitalist orgy of the self, recognizing no profit but that which pads the economy of their own egos.<br /><br />From an Anarcho-Syndicalist perspective, The Fountainhead represents a kind of postmodern self-obsession, a fairy tale trip into the delusion that anything we do is disconnected from other people, or somehow does not build upon the work (and ideas) of others.  It is actually a stark warning of what happens if you take the philosophy underlying  Capitalism and a totally free market economic system to its ultimate logical, supposedly amoral conclusion--a conclusion that assumes concepts like tangible socialprogress, the beneficence of economic Darwinism, and the complete realization and perfection of self.<br /><br />Even Rand's intentionally collectivist character, Toohey, amounts to nothing more than a self-centered, power hungry, deluded intellectual trapped inside his own notions of progress.  His altruism amounts to the benevolence of the dictator who seeks power only as a means to self-actualization.<br /><br />The basic tenants of Christianity are to love one another, to share resources (the fish and loaves), and to value the individual within the collective (as demonstrated complexly in the disciples' argument over who was the greatest among them).  There is not a character in Rand's story that comes remotely close to those ideals, nor one who uses his profits, be they monetary or intellectual, to further the collective health of his fellow men and women (and Toohey, least of them).<br /><br />The Fountainhead represents nihilism, greed and callousness; the very aspects of Capitalism that constantly call its moral foundation into question.<br /><br />###

B008OJ291W|5.0|The recordings on this collection are astounding aurally. Check it out with headphones. I have listened back and forth with the original tracks against the new tracks and it is great fun. First of all, Lynne has always been a great producer, but he is better now than he was earlier. The new recordings are cleaner and crisper. The arrangements as suggested in the original recordings are nailed solid in the new recordings. The sound is very full. Vocally, Lynne's voice may (miraculously) be better now. His phrasing sounds exactly as I remembered the originals but when you compare you find the new vocals actually nail the phrasing that was previously only suggested.<br /><br />Other interesting changes: the backup female vocals on songs like \\"Living Thing\\" have so much more \\"character.\\" The \\"band\\" sounds tighter on the new tracks: Listen to the intro to Mr. Blue: the old version has more space between the staccato piano part and the handclaps, the new version has them closer together and they are no longer handclaps, rather some other percussion, then the snare roll, again way cleaner, then the big cymbal crash--in the old version it is muddy and fades quickly, in the new version the first cymbal crash is bright and rings for almost 4 bars as it slowly fades away.<br /><br />Every single part has been recreated and in most cases has been done slightly better, crisper, tighter and recorded with more fidelity. The old versions, of course, used real strings and horns which always sounds better than synthesizers, although the new synthesized parts are done really well--but you can quibble there.<br /><br />The funny part is that if you just listen to it in the background on your car stereo, it's going to sound exactly the same--same old ELO. However -- if you got ears -- and wanna have some fun, you can get some good headphones and flip back and forth deconstructing these toons. I can't imagine enjoying this treatment with any of the other bands I love--imagine if Paul McCartney redid Sgt Pepper by himself: God!! No!!!--but somehow Lynne pulls it off here.

William's days of experimenting with new techniques and sounds are over and I have been critical of him as of late.<br /><br />Between his recent War Horse and Tintin soundtracks, Lincoln is the best. He establishes a signature theme and plays with it effortlessly. I was privileged with being able to see an early screening of the movie and the soundtrack is almost exclusively played softly for a subdued background effect. Unlike the Patriot, which I consider this score to be most similar to, the score is not the focal point. The most powerful moments of the film are times when there is no score being played at all.<br /><br />The main theme is really only played in the ending credits thus making this soundtrack almost better as a stand alone listen. Overall, I was moved by the score and it truly demonstrates the work of a god amongst men, yet it falls short of my admittedly lofty expectations.

These quirky few men pore over documents, research and interviews to discover that bundled subprime mortgage bonds are bolstered by false ratings--and then cloned into ghost-like copies to increase this falsely-bolstered market. On their way to enlightenment and to enlightening others, their ah-ha moments are laugh-out-loud funny.<br /><br />Which brings me to the most important thing about this book: the sand-like ignorance that was the foundation of the Wall Street institutions that buckled in 2008. The film recreates the episode associated with the rescue of six American diplomats, who managed to escape from the U.S. Embassy in Tehran during the assault and seizure of the embassy by Iranian militant students-rebels on Nov. 4, 1979, and who found refuge in the residence of the Canadian Ambassador at the time, Ken Taylor. CIA in conjunction with Canadian government had come up with an ingenious and risky operation in saving and extracting the six from Tehran from which even a bird would not be able to fly undetected. The more I read the more I realized that this book was written by someone that had experienced what he was talking about and talked about it with a degree of clarity that can only come from clarity of thought, and this clarity of thought can only come to rare individuals that are able, as he says in his book, distance themselves for the thought process itself--hence as he says, the root of all creative acts.  This book is wonderful: clear and profound, and highly recommended. It says on the back of the book that \\"He writes with the timeless and uncomplicated clarity of the ancient spiritual masters.\\"  Like who? They should have been able to write with this clarity. I suppose it has to do with the times in which they wrote but who wrote with clarity? The interpreters of the Buddha's talks? My beloved but nearly unreadable Plotinus? Meister Eckhart and other unreadable, \\"local\\"-interpreting Christian mystics, with the exception of a few, like St. Teresa of Avila? This book has a deceptive simplicity to it. If you are well read in the mysticism of the East and or West, Mr. Tolle's words will resonate with even more meaning, but I am quite sure that this would not be his goal. It would be simply to understand what he is talking about in an immediate, personal way. The show held at City Hall in Hull is the very same set as Leeds and the tapes show Keith came out of the gate with John's tune: \\"Heaven & Hell\\" and made his humble self known right away as a one man wrecking machine behind his drumkit. \\"I Can't Explain\\" is a song The Who could play in their sleep and it carries all the power you would expect from a band that spent most of 1968 & 1969 on tour perfecting the greatest stage act in the business. In the \\"Ivor\\" section Keith starts a roll you hope won't ever let up and he is nailing this show as he is the biggest thing in the mix. This is the best way to hear \\"Tommy\\" as it gets to breathe new life in concert and this is as great as it gets.<br /><br />\\"Live At Hull - 1970\\" has been called a: \\"Warts 'N' All\\" release and it ain't perfect by any means but rock music cannot be and should not be perfect to be great. Rock music becomes something all-together different when The Who performed live between 1968-1971 as they soar above the planet as the best of the best. 0783226063|5.0|It's too bad director David Lynch was in over his head on this one. This was the first (and last) time he worked with a big budget, attempting to adapt a novel that, in paperback form, is over 700 pages. Clocking in at under two-and-a-half hours, \\"Dune\\" is highly compressed and inconsistent in its pacing, and yet it is a deeply compelling and incredibly immersive work of art.<br /><br />\\"Dune\\" opens with Princess Irulan, who provides some basic exposition: It is the year 10,191, and a regressive, feudalistic society known as the Imperium (overseen by Irulan's father, the Emperor) holds a monopoly over a spice known as melange. The spice can only be found on the desert planet Arrakis, or Dune, and exposure to it extends life, consciousness and grants limited prescient powers, which mutants known as guild navigators use to guide star ships through interstellar journeys.<br /><br />House Atreides (say it with me: Uh-tray-dees), who belong to the so-called great houses, poses a threat to the Emperor. He wishes to eliminate them by relocating them to Dune, where they will oversee spice production, at which point their enemies, House Harkonnen, will launch a surprise attack. This plan falls short, however, as the Atreides son, Paul (Kyle MacLachlan), and his mother, Jessica, manage to flee to the desert, where they find sanctuary with the desert people known as the Fremen. Brutal, strong-willed and religiously fanatic, they regard Paul and Jessica as messianic figures. Paul uses his new position of power to train the Fremen to battle against their Harkonnen oppressors, as well as halt spice production so as to force the Emperor to deal with him directly.<br /><br />That is only a basic summary, which doesn't even delve into the massive, destructive sandworms that are of incredible importance to the Fremen culture, the frightening mental powers that Jessica and the sisterhood known as the Bene Gesserit have bestowed upon Paul, the bizarre form of combat known as the \\"weirding way\\" which is an invention of Lynch's, or the fact that both Paul and his mother must consume the poisonous \\"water of life\\" in order to elevate their status among the Fremen. As you can imagine, trying to cram that and so much more into a slightly longer than average running time is exceedingly difficult, and at times it shows rather obviously. Entire chapters of the book are removed or reduced to a single scene. This is in direct contrast to segments of the film that are intentionally slow burning. Despite these fluctuations, Dune rewards its viewers with repeat viewings.<br /><br />In particular, the costume and set design, which is simultaneously futuristic and ancient, is far superior to that of the SyFy Channel miniseries from 2000, and can only truly be appreciated after you've seen the film a good three or four times. Both the sound and music are typical of a Lynch movie; brilliantly memorable with plenty of low hums, although it's a shame that Brian Eno only contributed a single track, the emotionally moving \\"Prophecy Theme.\\" Lynch really went the extra mile to include specific details from the novel, and author Frank Herbert even commented that some of the characters were portrayed more favorably on screen than off. While knowledge of the book certainly helps in appreciating such details, anyone who is a fan of surreal filmmaking will be dazzled by how gracefully \\"Dune\\" has aged, although the green screening and space folding scenes are exceedingly dated. Anyone who enjoys unconventional science fiction will find this picture incredibly satisfying, unless you're the type who nit-picks about creative liberties, or insists on straightforward storytelling. In that case, stick to \\"Star Wars.\\"

I also feel that this game has not received enough credit on its Amazon port, as very few people know about it. In an attempt to give an accurate and easy to view review, here are the pros and cons of the puzzle game \\"Totemo HD\\":<br /><br />Pros -<br />*Great Visuals - The graphics in \\"Totemo HD\\" are what puts this puzzle game over the top for me. On the Kindle Fire HD, the visuals are breathtaking due to its usage of the High Definition version. The background visuals, the menu screens, and the spirits themselves bring an eerie feeling to this puzzle game. I would say that these visuals bring a feel reminiscent of the zen puzzle game \\"Quell\\". Your \\"guide\\" and narrator of the game (the totem man; traveling between worlds) is very vibrant and again adds to the ghastly feel of the game.<br />*Easy Controls; Difficult Game - \\"Totemo HD\\" paces the game well as you begin to play it by matching the \\"spirits\\" in pairs of various numbers (2, 3, ect.) in vertical or horizontal lines. The 100 levels in this game will keep you going for a while, and the challenge makes it worthwhile in comparison to some puzzle games that are just too easy for a majority of the time.<br />*Great Accompaniment Soundtrack - The Kindle Fire HD's Dolby Digital Plus sound system seems to make great use of Hexage's soundtrack in their games, and the eerie sounds of \\"Totemo HD\\" add to the general feel of the game. Regular Kindle Fire users will not be able to use the HD aspect of this game, sadly, and I would suggest if possible, Hexage, to put a regular port of the game on the marketplace.<br />*Possibly Short - \\"Totemo HD\\" currently has 100 levels. I have heard that it previously had only 62, and I am glad that Hexage expounded on the number of levels. To many, this seems to be too few levels, but the difficulty of them (starting VERY early on) makes it longer than one may expect. Even so, I can't imagine this game taking too long for people to beat, but I would recommend playing survival mode or the story again.<br />*No hints - There is no hint system or \\"pay-to-proceed\\" system using either real money or in game currency (as there is no in game currency). This may turn some people off, since the levels are difficult. For me, this wasn't a problem.<br /><br />Conclusion -<br />\\"Totemo HD\\" is a puzzle game that is hard to pass up. It's challenging, beautiful, and the soundtrack is great for doing these puzzles. I highly recommend this game, and I feel it has gotten far to little recognition on the Amazon Appstore. Check this game out, even if you are only a casual puzzle gamer.

It reminds me of the Matrix Trilogy where 1 & 2 were a wonderful set up for part 3, and we were all left speechless when we realized that the best answer the filmmakers had to all those puzzling, intriguing questions was a big ass gun fight. There is a lot to learn from in this film. Dariusz Wolski's cinematography is quite spectacular, and the production design is extremely well done. B009VB97EY|4.0|Magic Mike got a lot of hype for being a 'stripper film'. However, watching the actual film, it's very deeply thought out.<br /><br />Magic Mike doesn't want to glamourise stripping, nor does it want to be critical, it just wants to portray modern culture as it really is. The film centres around Mike and Adam. Mike is 30, seasoned and confident, while Adam is 18, lacks life experience and is a petty thief. Mike takes Adam under his wing in the stripping industry, teaching him 'techniques'. They work for a guy in a cowboy hat called 'Dallas'. They meet some very interesting people.<br /><br />The movie is a good reflection of society as it is today, with people obsessed about golden bodies in night clubs, appearance and celebrity; not trying to give the culture a bad image, just trying to portray things as they really are.<br /><br />The most interesting scene, however, is when Mike tries to get a loan off the bank. He tries all his smooth-talk and techniques on the lady behind the counter, but none of it works.<br /><br />Overall, an interesting movie where everyone gets what they, want, without a queasy happy ending, well, almost. I'd rate it 7 or 8 out of 10. Good quality viewing.

B009A9EPLM|5.0|If I could have one wish come true, it would be that I will enjoy this Motion Picture, as much as I enjoy this Music.<br /><br />I awoke this morning with John Williams' rousing score for \\"The Cowboys\\" in my head.  That's a beautiful theme and I hadn't hummed it in many months, but knew every note by heart.  There hasn't been a day since June 1977 that I haven't had at least one of John Williams' scores playing in my head, or hummed on my lips.<br /><br />\\"Lincoln.\\"  This is music which should be listened to in the evening hours.  In a dimly lit room, shades drawn.  With a glass of wine and candles lit, you'll soon fall into an ambiance of love and remembrance, traveling back 148 years to 1864.<br /><br />Now about Lincoln, the man.  We know how the story ends.  It's tragic.  Lincoln was a tragic figure in American history.  A great leader, wise and courageous and a mystery of how he comported himself and sounded in real life.  Maestro Williams segregates (forgive the pun) Lincoln in a magnificent aura of haunting melodies to portray the President as a hallowed figure, and a leader who loved America.  This is important in fully appreciating this masterful score.<br /><br />Performed by the Chicago Symphony Orchestra and Chorus, the music is utterly flawless and beautiful.  Shawn Murphy has been recording and mixing John Williams' scores for many years, and he deserves much appreciation from Williams' fans when it comes to tweaking digital music to perfection.  Thank you, Shawn!<br /><br />\\"The People's House\\" reminded me just a little of some passages from \\"NIXON\\" with a fading solo trumpet, however this is all original and different from anything I have heard from Maestro Williams.  It is unbelievably new and you will be moved by it.<br /><br />\\"Getting Out The Vote\\" will have you tapping your toes to the beat of this great little fiddle melody.  This is wonderfully folksy, you're ears will be grinning from this one.<br /><br />\\"The Southern Delegation And The Dream.\\"  An incredibly moving and fanciful selection.  Nobody does it better than the Maestro.<br /><br />\\"The Race To The House.\\"  A period-piece, country lively and city robust that makes me want to see the movie.<br /><br />If there is anything missing in this score (but perfectly understandable) is the scarce use of percussion instruments.  In all of the symphonic universe, John Williams is the greatest composer who ever lived to master the use of the percussion section in an orchestra.  He must have inherited this from his father.  But \\"Call To Muster\\" is satisfying in this regard, and the chorus singing \\"Battle Cry Of Freedom,\\" will make the hair on the back of your neck stand at attention!<br /><br />Perhaps something else missing which I would have loved to hear, would have been the Chicago Symphony and Chorus, conducted by the Maestro doing \\"The Battle Hymn Of The Republic,\\" included as a bonus on this CD. B000035P7X|5.0|Don't Look Back is one of the best and most inspiring music documentaries I have ever seen.  It captures a young Bob Dylan during the height of his fame on a three week tour of London in 1965.  The performance footage in this film is some of the last of Dylan before he went electric months later at Newport Folk Festival.  The director, D.A. Pennebaker manages to remain a quiet, fly on the wall, throughout the entire film.  To any filmmaker or video artist who works in an observant, cinema verite style, this film is your bible.  Pennebaker does not interfere with any of the scenes or people.  He does not hold any interviews with Dylan, friends, or fans.  He just stands there with his camera, quietly soaking up everything like a sponge.  There are appearances made by Joan Baez, who at the time was seeing Dylan, Alan Price, right after he left The Animals, and Donovan, who appears to intimidate Dylan at certain moments in the film.  The film also includes famous beat poet, Allen Ginsberg, and Dylan's sidekick throughout the tour, Bob Neuwirth.  Pennebaker does not show as much concert footage as you think most music documentaries would, however, he includes more music than any music documentary ever could.  When Dylan isn't on stage performing, he is in the hotel room writing or singing a song, whether it be by himself or in the company of ten other people, including Donovan.  It is extremely inspiring to see how Dylan's life is simply all about music and how truly passionate he is about it.

0553381407|5.0|If you are locked inside family dynamics, and are confused by what you think is the right thing to do--or if you are struggling with guilt because what you feel or have to say upsets others in your life--this is a book you should read.<br /><br />When we are born our minds are clean slates.  We don't realize that our perceptions about the world, and the rules we are being taught to play by are actually forms of programming.<br /><br />If mommy uses guilt to get us to do what she wants--we learn to believe that is appropriate--right and good--and that mommy has all the power in the world to manipulate us into doing what she would prefer us to do.<br /><br />In my world--because I am an adult child of two adult children of alcoholics...I call this codependency.  But in reality--it is truly a toxic dynamic--regardless of what any mental health professional classifies it as.<br /><br />Anytime we forgo our own needs, wants, or opinions for the sake of keeping others calm, happy, or liking us--we are participating in a codependent and toxic dynamic.<br /><br />The interesting thing about this book--is how simply the information is presented.  Even if you never realized you were being manipulated...or bent to the will of others...after reading this book you may be surprised at how ambiguous toxicity can be.<br /><br />When we are little we do not have the cognitive ability to understand when mommy and daddy are making us responsible for their feelings...Then--when we get older, and bring spouses into our lives--the manipulation is sometimes much easier to see.  Toxic families lie, deceive and play mind games,  They shield themselves from the outside worlds, and know how to play the game so not to bring too much attention to them.  This sometimes makes it very difficult for children of toxic parents to 'see' that something is wrong within the family dynamic.<br /><br />I enjoyed this book for the empowering message it gives.  It helped me see things that I--even as a one who has considered herself in emotional recovery for sometime--missed.<br /><br />It is not easy to see our families truth and to come face to face with the fact that the parents we adore--are hurting us.  We want so much to love them and be loved by them...that we ignore the shameful ways they make us feel just for the sake of holding onto some kind of a relationship.<br /><br />When we finally face our parents dysfunctional belief systems...it is frightening and confusing.  We must learn to grieve who we thought our parents were, and what we will never have.<br /><br />It is not easy...but it is the only way to finally be free...<br /><br />Namaste...<br /><br />Lisa A.
